    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1825226549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1825226549" />
    <a title="stormin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3809399-stormin"><img alt="stormin" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1825226549?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="stormin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="stormin" href="/user/show/3809399-stormin">stormin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3809399-stormin?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1825226549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3960406423949813900">The conclusion to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu' title='Cixin Liu' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cixin Liu</a>'s much-vaunted sci-fi trilogy is a profoundly ambitious and deeply flawed work that came as a great let-down after the first two books, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___1_' title='The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #1) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Three-Body Problem</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_' title='The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dark Forest</a>. Part of that is probably expectation. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___1_' title='The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #1) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Three-Body Problem</a> was heavily hyped in 2014 and--when it won the Hugo for Best Novel that contentious year--its star grew even brighter as a kind of savior of what had otherwise turned into a nasty, contentious, depressing award season. I loved that</span>
  <span id="freeText3960406423949813900" style="display:none">The conclusion to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu" title="Cixin Liu" rel="nofollow noopener">Cixin Liu</a>'s much-vaunted sci-fi trilogy is a profoundly ambitious and deeply flawed work that came as a great let-down after the first two books, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___1_" title="The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #1) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three-Body Problem</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_" title="The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forest</a>. Part of that is probably expectation. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___1_" title="The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #1) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three-Body Problem</a> was heavily hyped in 2014 and--when it won the Hugo for Best Novel that contentious year--its star grew even brighter as a kind of savior of what had otherwise turned into a nasty, contentious, depressing award season. I loved that book, and in my eagerness I read lots of previews and reviews and background and--somewhere along the way--I read that it was the <em>third</em> novel (this one, Death's End) that had really turned the books into a sensation in China.<br /><br />The second book--<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_" title="The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forest</a>--was a bit of a let-down for me, but it had a really, really strong ending that got my hopes up once again. <br /><br />But then something odd happened: it seemed like everyone suddenly stopped talking about the series. I didn't see a single blog post, review, Tweet, Facebook status, or anything for Death's End. I just went and checked on my own and found that, yes: it was available already. <br /><br />That comparative radio silence is pretty easy to explain. <br /><br />You see: Americans can get pretty solipsistic about our politics. We have pretty clear conceptions of left and right, and we divide our entire country along those lines, more or less, with maybe a little bit of a libertarian also-ran along the fringes. This leads to all kinds of fairly silly misunderstandings when we encounter strong, international views and accidentally assume that they line up with our own, not realizing that the political divides in other countries are often very, very different from those in our own nation. Different nations care about different topics (for one thing) and draw their identities in different ways (for another). The group that most readily embraced <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___1_" title="The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #1) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three-Body Problem</a> initially was the American left, which viewed the prominent success of a Chinese novel as an exemplar of progressive multiculturalism and open-mindedness. Unfortunately, they didn't pay very close attention to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu" title="Cixin Liu" rel="nofollow noopener">Cixin Liu</a>'s political views, which include some (by American standards) incredibly regressive views on gender. Those views were present in the first book, but fairly subtle. They were more evident in the second book, but it's in the third book where the association of femininity with maternalism, compassion, passivity, and (from a certain point of view) weakness become completely impossible to ignore because they are one of the focal points of the book. Now, to my mind <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu" title="Cixin Liu" rel="nofollow noopener">Cixin Liu</a> is no sexist or misogynist: his book has vibrant female characters who are brilliant and easily pass the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bechdel_test" rel="nofollow noopener">Bechdel test</a> and all that jazz, but there's just no way that the book could be embraced by the same people who touted <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___1_" title="The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #1) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three-Body Problem</a> as a kind of anti-white supremacy high water mark. They failed to appreciate the fact that a Chinese author writing in Chinese for a Chinese audience was not going to map neatly onto American political categories. <br /><br />So I think that's why the book has attracted such a relatively low amount of attention. Everybody was talking about The Three Body Problem in 2014-2015. I haven't found <em>anybody</em> talking about Death's End in 2016.<br /><br />But that's not really why I disliked the book.<br /><br />My problem was mainly one of frustration.<br /><br />::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::<br />:: VAGUE SPOILERS TO FOLLOW ::<br />::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::::<br /><br />There are two points in this book where humanity is basically doomed. Both of them hinge on the decision of a single character. And in both cases she makes the wrong decision for fairly stupid reasons. This is--as a reader--basically unendurable.<br /><br />The first one is somewhat plausible: there's a standoff between humanity and the Tri-Solarans based on the willingness of humanity to commit suicide and take the Tri-Solarans with them rather than allow themselves to be conquered. This hinges on a single human being who is willing to press the red button, as it were, in the event of a Tri-Solaran attack. As you can imagine, this takes a particular kind of psychology, and for decades a steely-eyed warrior (the hero of the second book) fulfills this obligation. His threat is credible. But then--when he gets old--the decadent humans pass over 6 similarly aggressive candidates (all defined by their conventional masculinity) in favor of a 7th candidate who is defined by her conventional feminity. The Tri-Solarans correctly assess her as incapable of being willing to press the button, and launch a full-scale attack within seconds of the transfer of power. Sure enough: she collapses into tears and refuses to press the button, the Tri-Solaran attack succeeds (mostly) and humanity is imprisoned without food or water on Australia and given three months of cannibalism to reduce the population by 99.9% (or whatever). Now, they avoid this fate, but only through factors totally irrelevant to this character's decision and job. <br /><br />That's kind of hard to read, and it was during that point that I took a break to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24313624.Undeath_and_Taxes__Fred__the_Vampire_Accountant___2_" title="Undeath and Taxes (Fred, the Vampire Accountant, #2) by Drew Hayes" rel="nofollow noopener">Undeath and Taxes</a> to sort of clear my pallette.<br /><br />Little did I know, but it gets worse! <br /><br />For various reasons, this character is called upon again to decide the fate of humanity. This time the conflict is that a faction of humanity is developing light-speed travel as a means of escaping the solar system when unknown hostile aliens blow up the sun. The rest of humanity believes such research is dangerous, and is content to ride out the destruction of the sun by hiding in the solar shadow of Jupiter, Saturn, and Neptune. The faction developing light speed gives this character the final say: do they continue to fight for their right to research another route of escape, or do they capitulate? She orders them--without a moment's hestitation--to completely and totally capitulate, abandon all the research, and turn over all their weapons. Not only are a great many of them executed for following her instructions but, sure enough, when the attack comes it turns out that hiding in the shadow of the gas giants <em>doesn't</em> save humanity, and the entire species is wiped out because they didn't develop light-speed travel in time.<br /><br />So, the same character dooms humanity <em>twice</em> in the same book, and the second time was <b>incredibly</b> egregious because--as a reader--it is more than patently obvious that the plan to hide in the shadow of the gas giants <em>won't work</em>. It makes no sense, and what's more there is ample precedent <em>within this series</em> that it won't work. <br /><br />For me, these two events were basically deal-breakers that made it impossible to enjoy the book. I get that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu" title="Cixin Liu" rel="nofollow noopener">Cixin Liu</a> did a lot of retroactive attempts to justify the plot, but for me the attempts fell completely flat. <br /><br />So, why three stars?<br /><br />Because <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu" title="Cixin Liu" rel="nofollow noopener">Cixin Liu</a> also did some of the most brilliant far-future sci-fi writing that I have ever read in my life. No, I will take that back, it is unreservedly the <em>best</em> far-future sci-fi I've ever read, and really about the only far-future sci-fi that I enjoyed at all. Something that often bugs me is reading sci-fi ostensibly set in the far, far distant future and yet involving plots and characters that are not even <em>slightly</em> different from contemporary society. (For one such example: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21944107.Coming_Home__Alex_Benedict___7_" title="Coming Home (Alex Benedict, #7) by Jack McDevitt" rel="nofollow noopener">Coming Home</a>) When the attempts aren't just superificial to the point of being paper-thin, they are often impenetrable and boring. (I'm not going to provie an example here because the book in question was one I gave up on when I was a teenager, and I'd like to take a second look before using it as the poster child here.)<br /><br />By contrast, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15424505.Cixin_Liu" title="Cixin Liu" rel="nofollow noopener">Cixin Liu</a> thought very, very seriously about what society might look like--not just for humans but for all sentient life--over a very, very long time scale (billions of years). And then he wrote about it compellingly, creatively, and at times even beautifully. This book is chock-full of mind-blowing concepts, some of the coolest I've ever read in any sci-fi book, and I just can't give a book that includes such brilliance only 2-stars. <br /><br />I loved this book. I also hated it.<br /><br />If you're interested in sci-fi, I encourage you to read it too, even though I only gave it three stars, because the three stars can't convey the combination of very high highs and very low lows that you will encounter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3960406423949813900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1546686869" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1546686869" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30706026-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465615148p2/30706026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1546686869?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/30706026-gary">Gary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1546686869" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13773566084723749577">It has been more than two weeks since I finished reading the third book in Cixin Liu's Three Body Trilogy, and it has left me with a lot to process. It would be impossible to cover everything I want to say about this book into one review. Among those things, I recently had a discussion (in my GR review of The Dark Forest - check it out along with the comments if you're interested) about Liu's conservative Marxism, and I won't rehash that here.<br/>This is also a five star review for a novel that I ha</span>
  <span id="freeText13773566084723749577" style="display:none">It has been more than two weeks since I finished reading the third book in Cixin Liu's Three Body Trilogy, and it has left me with a lot to process. It would be impossible to cover everything I want to say about this book into one review. Among those things, I recently had a discussion (in my GR review of The Dark Forest - check it out along with the comments if you're interested) about Liu's conservative Marxism, and I won't rehash that here.<br />This is also a five star review for a novel that I have some very emphatic philosophical differences with. Liu's views on gender types and gender roles are traditional, to say the least. If you were taken aback (as I was) by the female characters in Three Body Problem and Dark Forest, you will be positively incensed by Death's End. The angelic Cheng Xin - the woman at the center of the novel's events - is alternately put on a pedestal and torn off of it, and constantly patronized and coddled by the male characters. The "feminization" of human values in Death's End always leads to humanity's peril. <br />To be fair though, Liu's depressingly cynical outlook has little use for masculine values either. If the feminine is regressive and fetal, the masculine is belligerent and destructive, and the oscillation of these extremes is the fatal cocktail for the disaster that befalls the human race in Death's End. For Liu, there is no escape from our basic nature, unless... (see discussion of Marxism in my Dark Forest review/comments).<br />The scope of Liu's imagination and the weight of his intellectual enterprise makes my distaste for his philosophical positions palatable. I don't need to agree with you to appreciate you, so long as you can back your shit up. Liu backs his with a universe where time and space itself can be toys or weapons (or both); where you can literally reach through the fourth dimension to interact with yourself in the past; where one can collapse an entire solar system (or universe) into two dimensions as easily as sneezing into a tissue. No other science fiction writer today - not even the greatest of them - is coming close to operating on his level. This is the kind of thing that must be read and discussed by conscientious readers everywhere, and only the highest praise will do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13773566084723749577" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1827224718" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1827224718" />
    <a title="Stevie Kincade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56187822-stevie-kincade"><img alt="Stevie Kincade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476505168p2/56187822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1827224718?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stevie Kincade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stevie Kincade" href="/user/show/56187822-stevie-kincade">Stevie Kincade</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=published-2016">published-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56187822-stevie-kincade?shelf=best-of-2016">best-of-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1827224718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9231429052287393163">(Audiobook) Death's End is a whopper of a story in size, scope and length (29 hours!). The book is overflowing with ideas and stacked to the upper limit of the 3rd dimension with thoughtful SF concepts. This is the first book in the series that didn't suffer from having crappy characters to follow because there is enough going on here that the universe itself can become the main character. <br/><br/>If you haven't started this series, or you read "3 body" the big question is <i> should I read this giant arse</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9231429052287393163" style="display:none">(Audiobook) Death's End is a whopper of a story in size, scope and length (29 hours!). The book is overflowing with ideas and stacked to the upper limit of the 3rd dimension with thoughtful SF concepts. This is the first book in the series that didn't suffer from having crappy characters to follow because there is enough going on here that the universe itself can become the main character. <br /><br />If you haven't started this series, or you read "3 body" the big question is <i> should I read this giant arse trilogy? </i><br /><br />To which I am giving a definitive and resounding <i> maybe </i><br /><br />I thought the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1672165454" rel="nofollow noopener">3 body problem</a> was different, flawed but interesting. It is a very well done Audiobook, it clocks in at around 16 hours.<br /><br />I have a very strong dislike towards <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1811112158" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forrest</a> which only grew after reading "Death's End". "The Dark Forrest" could have been a 5 star short story or novella. The concept behind it is brilliant. Instead it was 23 hours of waffling nonsense and ill considered plot holes leading up to a great speech/ concept reveal. <br />My recommendation would be to read a plot summary of "The Dark Forrest" and save yourself 23 hours. Know what Luo Ji's story is, what the Dark Forrest is and that is really all you need to know for Death's End. We spend at least 7 hours on Luo Ji's imaginary girlfriend come to life and in "Death's End" <b> We get 2 sentences about her leaving Luo Ji and she is never mentioned again!</b> Way to payoff that storyline! <br /><br />So this series is a HUGE time commitment. You could read Baxter's entire Xeelee sequence AND another regular sized book in the time it would take to read these 3 (possibly you should). Death's End is pretty damn great though so I will do my best to run down what was good and not so good with it.<br /><br />While I am on the subject of Baxter I HAVE to believe the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1681479796" rel="nofollow noopener">Xeelee sequence</a> is a huge influence on this series. Possibly it is just a case of "intelligent minds think alike" but there are more parallels between the 2 then could be listed here and seem mere coincidence. Liu's trilogy is more colourful and philosophical, Baxter has better hard science porn and a LOT less problems with plot holes etc. <br /> <br />"The Dark Forrest" started so damn slow. Thankfully "Death's End" starts really fast and never lets up (or I would have bailed). "The deterrence era" is the period where Trisloaris and humanity are deadlocked. I loved this section of the book, it was so well thought out. Then the idea on 4 dimensional space and the entities and weapons that go between dimensions - phenomenal. <br /><br />If you live in New York or LA or Beijing you have probably seen or read your home city being blown up in dozens of books and movies. The whole "Australia sequence" was great and having my home city of Sydney be the city wiped off of the Earth was a blast. The way he described it going down with Australia's multicultural but xenophobic attitudes rang sadly true as well.<br />Overall the best thing about the book is the MASSIVE scope and ideas. You have to straight up respect the mind that came up with them. RESPECT Liu!<br /><br />Onto the not-so-good:<br />In this episode of <i>Cixin Liu's bizarre issues with women</i> we have a heroic male character who creepily stalks the object of his desires, never speaking to her but believing she telepathically understands him (she does!). Then we (finally) have a woman rising to the important security office of "Sword holder" and within 30 minutes of her taking over - the aliens wreck shit up because they know she "views life as a fairytale" (I shit you not!). After all Liu's crimes against female characters he practically flops his old fellow out, craps on the lawn, then pimp slaps feminism in "Death's End". Liu sees the maternal, "soft" side as a fatal flaw for humanity although he seems frightened of overt masculinity too. As mentioned above Lui Ji's family (a giant part of the 2nd book) get written off in 2 sentences making their whole arc absolutely useless.<br /><br />There was a very strange section where Chung Shin is befriended by an Aboriginal elder. He didn't quite let her play the didgeridoo or tell her dreamtime stories (taboo for women) but in a completely bizarre scene he told her she was confusing Aboriginals with Maoris, and he then preceded to don Maori face paint and dance the haka. This would be like Chung Shin visiting a Peruvian Shaman, mistaking him for a Mexican after which he says "I'm not Mexican" before donning a sombrero and playing "La Cucaracha".<br /><br />The worst part of the book was midway through where the Trisloarans let a human tell a loooooooooooooooooooooong arse fairytale. Would we let the worst Al Quaeda terrorist tell a 2 hour Afghani traditional faiytale to the Taliban knowing he was probably coding some intelligence within it? Well the Trisolarans did, right after Liu tells the reader that they now understood and practiced subterfuge themselves. Even worse than the logic issue was that the pace was really cracking up until that point. In the audiobook the fairytale is recounted in <i> painstaking detail </i> for nearly 2 hours! KILLING the momentum. The narrator decided to voice the fairytale characters in over the top cockney voices which didn't help. The "decoding of the Fairytale" was well done but the story itself was a SLOG. This was bad but compared to the problems in "The Dark Forrest" it was only a speed bump.<br /><br />I've mentioned a few plot points but they are all from quite early in the book. The middle and end take a lot of twists and turns. With 2 hours left I thought there was a pretty decent option for an ending but instead we went through the wringer one last time. I don't know if the ending was AMAZING but it was a solid conclusion to one of the most ambitious BIG IDEA SF's of the year.<br /><br />Narrator PJ Ochlan returns from "The Dark Forrest". He is above average and had a much easier time with considerably less characters to voice in "Death's End". His Aboriginal accent was shite but granted that is a hard one to do. I don't know why he wanted to do the Fairytale voices as "Eric Idle and his cockney friends" but I am quibbling, he is fine. <br /><br />This is not a light read but highly recommended for fans of Hard-SF and Galaxy spanning space opera. <br /><br /> <br /><br /><br /> <br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9231429052287393163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2297852651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2297852651" />
    <a title="Jody " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41767362-jody"><img alt="Jody " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558120407p2/41767362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2297852651?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jody " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jody " href="/user/show/41767362-jody">Jody </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780765386632" href="/book/show/33517569-death-s-end">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41767362-jody?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41767362-jody?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2297852651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16452606903414141332">In the following review I will try my best to explain just how grand of scale and pure genius this trilogy truly is. Liu Cixin is a master at taking the scientific and technological world we live in today and transcending it through the ages. It has a <b>classic feel</b> mixed with <b>new age knowledge</b> and <b>futuristic ideas</b>. <br/><br/>This has been an adventure that took root in the Chinese Cultural Revolution of the 1960's and has traveled through countless eras, centuries, and millennia. Characters have come and g</span>
  <span id="freeText16452606903414141332" style="display:none">In the following review I will try my best to explain just how grand of scale and pure genius this trilogy truly is. Liu Cixin is a master at taking the scientific and technological world we live in today and transcending it through the ages. It has a <b>classic feel</b> mixed with <b>new age knowledge</b> and <b>futuristic ideas</b>. <br /><br />This has been an adventure that took root in the Chinese Cultural Revolution of the 1960's and has traveled through countless eras, centuries, and millennia. Characters have come and gone with a lasting impression or a brief blink of the eye. To take a trilogy and have a different main character in each book is a bit unusual. But Liu Cixin seems to do this easily. In <i>Death’s End</i>, our MC is Cheng Xin. She is an aerospace engineer from the 21st century. Through Cheng Xin, the reader follows the ups and downs of humanity from one era to the next. From a technological boom to moments of almost certain doom, her life’s story is one of incredible adventure and the deepest sorrow.<br /> <br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"I am but an ordinary person. Unfortunately, I have not been able to walk the ordinary person’s path. My path is, in reality, the journey of a civilization.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />Speaking of technology! The technology displayed in <i>Death’s End</i> is <b>
  <u>amazing</u>
</b>. Underground societies, multi-dimensional weapons, and space travel are only a few that stand out. Each one is detailed and the reader is given information on each to describe how they work. Not that I understood it all, but it was really cool to read about. Not only describing the technology, but how future societies function within it is also key to any good sci-fi novel. This is also done very well. Liu Cixin details the society and the society structure. Not too much in detail to feel like an info dump, but just enough to give you an overall feel of how the society works. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>”Progress begot more progress; countless miracles gave birth to more miracles; humankind seemed to possess the power of gods; but in the end, the real power was wielded by time.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />There were many incredible scenes throughout this book and trilogy. It is conveyed in a way that makes it feel so real that I know I will never look at our galaxy the same way again. The story has developed and expanded so much from book 1 to book 3, it feels like I have read an entire series instead of just a trilogy. This has been <b>
  <u>by far</u>
</b> the <b>
  <u>most authentic</u>
</b> and <b>
  <u>imaginative</u>
</b> sci-fi story I have read to date. If one of you out there has read this trilogy and does not agree with my last statement, please let me know what you consider to be better than this. I would love to try it!<br /><br />This has been an amazing adventure, and I can’t thank my friend Petrik enough for urging me to start this right away. I will do the same to you fellow readers. Even if you are not a sci-fi fan I would urge you to give this a try. Just for the pure honesty and imagination it portrays for the human race and our future. Even if it is fiction, it’s still one hell of a story. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>”But even more worrisome was the greatest lure provided by the future: the end of death.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16452606903414141332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2995956683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2995956683" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3536004-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535136437p2/3536004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2995956683?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/3536004-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2995956683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15225423854346200313"><i>
  <b>"Death is the only lighthouse that is always lit. No matter where you sail, ultimately, you must turn toward it. Everything fades in the world, but Death endures.”</b>
</i><br/>​<br/>How the hell do you even review a book like this???? <b>It's mind-bogglingly mind-blowing!​</b> This is my favourite book of the Remembrance of the Earth's Past trilogy and the only thing I don't like about it is that it's the final one. Wow, what a ride this has been! <br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1570456935ra/28257771.gif' width='254' height='204' alt='Blown Away Mind Blow GIF - BlownAway MindBlow Omg GIFs' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I won't recap the book. If you've read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past__1_' title='The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past #1) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Three-Body Problem</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_' title='The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The</a></span>
  <span id="freeText15225423854346200313" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>"Death is the only lighthouse that is always lit. No matter where you sail, ultimately, you must turn toward it. Everything fades in the world, but Death endures.”</b>
</i><br>​<br>How the hell do you even review a book like this???? <b>It's mind-bogglingly mind-blowing!​</b> This is my favourite book of the Remembrance of the Earth's Past trilogy and the only thing I don't like about it is that it's the final one. Wow, what a ride this has been! <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1570456935ra/28257771.gif" width="254" height="204" alt="Blown Away Mind Blow GIF - BlownAway MindBlow Omg GIFs" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I won't recap the book. If you've read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20518872.The_Three_Body_Problem__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past__1_" title="The Three-Body Problem (Remembrance of Earth’s Past #1) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three-Body Problem</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_" title="The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forest</a> you know what it's about. If you've not read them, you don't know what you're missing out on! I know science fiction isn't for everyone, and this is some <i>serious</i> science fiction, not for the faint of heart. Lots of physics and explanations of things such as moving about in other dimensions, creating mini universes, and slowing down the speed of light, things which made even my head spin at times. It was utterly <b>thrilling</b> to read!<br><br>It's obvious English is not the original language and at times the writing feels awkward. Normally, that would put me off a book. However, these books are so <i>freaking</i> amazing that I could not care less about that! I don't think it's the fault of the translator; I think it's due to the different writing styles and dialogue of Eastern and Western writers/speakers. <br><br>This space-aged saga is easily one of the best I've ever read and probably the most imaginative, spanning millions of years and many universes. It is monumental. Liu Cixin is brilliant and this trilogy is truly out of this world. Highly recommend!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15225423854346200313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3394837620" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3394837620" />
    <a title="Henk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76202320-henk"><img alt="Henk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557871304p2/76202320.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3394837620?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henk" href="/user/show/76202320-henk">Henk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76202320-henk?shelf=owned-e-books">owned-e-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3394837620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1118988065407090750"><b>Addictive, grandiose and sweeping in ambition and scope. Like <i>Interstellar</i> on steroids in book form - 4.5 stars rounded up</b><br/><i>“No one should live if we all have to die!”</i><br/><br/><b>General and some critique</b><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25451264.Death_s_End__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past__3_' title='Death's End (Remembrance of Earth’s Past #3) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death's End</a> can not really be read and understood as a stand alone work. I already liked <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_' title='The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dark Forest</a> a lot but this conclusion of the Remembrance of Earth's Past in the end was even more epic and impressive.<br/><br/>Not to say that the writing of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5780686.Liu_Cixin' title='Liu Cixin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Liu Cixin</a> is without fault, there are sentimental statements like: <i>“</i></span>
  <span id="freeText1118988065407090750" style="display:none"><b>Addictive, grandiose and sweeping in ambition and scope. Like <i>Interstellar</i> on steroids in book form - 4.5 stars rounded up</b><br /><i>“No one should live if we all have to die!”</i><br /><br /><b>General and some critique</b><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25451264.Death_s_End__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past__3_" title="Death's End (Remembrance of Earth’s Past #3) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">Death's End</a> can not really be read and understood as a stand alone work. I already liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168817.The_Dark_Forest__Remembrance_of_Earth_s_Past___2_" title="The Dark Forest (Remembrance of Earth’s Past, #2) by Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forest</a> a lot but this conclusion of the Remembrance of Earth's Past in the end was even more epic and impressive.<br /><br />Not to say that the writing of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5780686.Liu_Cixin" title="Liu Cixin" rel="nofollow noopener">Liu Cixin</a> is without fault, there are sentimental statements like: <i>“The universe is grand, but life is grander. We’re certain to meet again.”</i><br />And even worse, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/630.Dan_Brown" title="Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">Dan Brown</a> like self congratulations on a few fairy tales around 2/3 of the book that really made me cringe, where the author makes some of his characters say about his own writing:<br /><i>As a writer, I want to express my respect for the author. As fairy tales, these are very good.”<br />&amp;<br />“A subtle and sophisticated system,” a PIA specialist said admiringly.</i><br /><br />And very conveniently somewhere around half of the novel there are sophon free rooms.<br />Finally I found the Australia section of the book lacking, it felt a lot like the Sounds of Drums episode of Doctor Who. Some ideas, like the Australia section, are so unimaginable that it would require something the length of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King" title="Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen King</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/149267.The_Stand" title="The Stand by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stand</a> to do right.<br /><br />But in the end this book truly blew me away through the sheer scope and boldness of the concepts contained in it, truly incomparable to anything I have read before and I used to be quite an avid scifi and fantasy reader in my teenage days.<br /><br /><b>Characterization and the protagonist</b><br />Characterization is not the strong suit of Cixin in my view.<br />Basically the author takes us on the struggle of humanity versus the dark forest of space, full of aliens, around it. And far from being a romantic struggle with a happy end this struggle is more like how ants would want to battle a human that is going to use bleach on their nest.<br />The below statement from protagonist Cheng Xin is in that sense rather ironic in my view:<br /><i>“I’m here with billions of people. No matter what happens, if it happens to several billion at the same time, it won’t be frightening.”</i><br /><br />Cheng Xin is put in kind of a similar position as the Wallfacers from the Dark Forest, with lots of hopes of humanity primed on her, but in my view for a large part of the book lacked personality. She sometimes felt like a puppet being moved by the author at will and need.<br />What is also not helpful is that she makes seemingly gross errors of kindness all the time, while humanity in its struggle with the Trisolarans seems to need a ruthless leader like her boss Wade, as she acknowledges herself:<br /><i>But he can get it done. This bastard, devil, murderer, careerist, political hooligan, technophilic madman... he can get it done.</i><br /><br />Also we have a romantic relationship between her and Tianmeng that transcends time and space and feels very reminiscent to Makoto Shikai's Voices of a Distant Star anime movie. <br />Which was an anime I liked, but is rather over the top for a novel, as kind of recognized by the author as well:<br /><i>“And he just made a romantic gesture that I’d call fucking ridiculous if I read it in a book or saw it in a movie.”</i><br /><br /><b>Themes and ideas</b><br /><i>When humans are lost in space, it takes only five minutes to reach totalitarianism.<br />vs<br />Humankind isn’t just some abstraction. To love humanity, you must start by loving individual persons, by fulfilling your responsibility to those you love.</i><br />One of the major themes of the book is the benefit of totalitarianism versus humanism and democracy. At some of the pivotal moments of the book you do wonder why a military dictatorship does not arise to fill the void in the power vacuum that arises after some of the major events covered in the novel.<br /><br />Scientific progress is another major component to the book, beautifully captured when some baby steps into a faster than light propulsion is being developed: <i>“You are saying that you’ve invented gunpowder and managed to make a firecracker, but the ultimate goal is to make a space rocket. A thousand years may separate those two achievements.”</i><br />I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1401.Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen Hawking</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3869.A_Brief_History_of_Time" title="A Brief History of Time by Stephen Hawking" rel="nofollow noopener">A Brief History of Time</a> as a twelve year old and loved it, but can very well imagine that someone less into physics would find the tale at points hard to get. <br />The nature and mallebility of lightspeed, the fundamental laws, quantum entanglement and dimensions is tackled, with the author even finding a brilliantly elegant explanation on the nature of dark matter. And the most epic destruction scene ever near the end of the book.<br />Somewhere it is remarked that <i>Time really was the cruelest force of all.</i> and even that is something you truly and viscerally understand as a reader.<br /><br />And then we have the literal world building itself when we follow the development of humanity in phases (<i>This wasn’t exactly regression, but a kind of spiraling ascent, a necessary condition for the exploration and settlement of new frontiers.</i>). Here the book shines and feels like a well done rendition of the last 1/3 of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22816087.Seveneves" title="Seveneves by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Seveneves</a>.<br />You feel the wonder of the space cities around the gas giants in the Bunker age, the feeling is almost tangible and contagious.<br /><br />All in all this is in the last part of the book blended together in such a way, so far from now, with a clear human touch, that I was very much in awe. <br /><br />It is easy to critique the book on some aspects as I did above. <br />It is very hard to capture the wonder and the feeling of being tiny that the author confer on me while reading.<br />And it is definitely a very great achievement of storytelling and writing by Liu Cixin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1118988065407090750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1805158871" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1805158871" />
    <a title="Matthew Quann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann"><img alt="Matthew Quann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528393487p2/22699228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1805158871?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Quann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Quann" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann">Matthew Quann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Sci-fi fans, seasoned readers, power-nerds</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=big-books-500-pages-plus">big-books-500-pages-plus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=not-can-us-or-uk">not-can-us-or-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1805158871" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15421449665755418254"><b>A cerebral, mind-expanding trilogy comes to a stunning conclusion in <i>Death's End</i></b>.<br/><br/>How do you manage to wrap up a sci-fi trilogy whose first two installments featured a Universe-spanning first-contact narrative, a philosophical investigation of humanity’s life post-Earth, and some seriously mind-bending concepts? If you’re Cixin Liu, then you just go bigger. <br/><br/>What’s perhaps most impressive about Liu’s work is that he is able to so accurately convey the unfathomable scale of space in his atypical se</span>
  <span id="freeText15421449665755418254" style="display:none"><b>A cerebral, mind-expanding trilogy comes to a stunning conclusion in <i>Death's End</i></b>.<br /><br />How do you manage to wrap up a sci-fi trilogy whose first two installments featured a Universe-spanning first-contact narrative, a philosophical investigation of humanity’s life post-Earth, and some seriously mind-bending concepts? If you’re Cixin Liu, then you just go bigger. <br /><br />What’s perhaps most impressive about Liu’s work is that he is able to so accurately convey the unfathomable scale of space in his atypical sequences. This works largely because Liu presents conflict on a galactic scale and makes the stakes intimately human. My only complain about <i>The Three-Body Problem</i> was that it suffered from weak characterization and was more focused on advancing its heady plot. This issue was resolved by <i>The Dark Forest</i>, which essentially worked as a character study of Luo Ji featuring all of the science-based goodies of the first installment. <br /><br /><i>Death’s End</i>, then, changes up the formula once again with a book that is conceptually heavier than its predecessors, but doesn’t lose sight of its human touchstones. As with the previous two volumes, <i>Death’s End</i> largely follows a new protagonist, Cheng Xin as she is thrust into an unexpected position of power in the Earth-Trisolaran conflict. Liu also makes use of ancillary characters and POV characters who are present only for single chapters. This alternating set of POVs makes for a book that often reads like a thriller where planets are murder victims and physics are the only hope at finding the killers.<br /><br />Speaking of killers, Liu holds nothing back in this final installment of the <i>Remembrance of Earth’s Past</i> and often left me reeling from the insane escalations in conflict. What happens in these 600 pages could easily have filled out two more novels. I thought this worked quite well in that it made me feel like the characters in the novel: constantly on the ropes against an ever-evolving set of opponents. <br /><br />Despite this relentless pace, <i>Death’s End</i> is easily the most challenging and rewarding installment in the trilogy. The hard sci-fi of the first two books is cranked all the way up to 11, making for a steep learning curve. It sometimes felt as if I was just getting a handle on a new concept and Liu would add another layer of complexity. It’s both the joy and frustration of this book that Liu is so intent on describing the universe he’s created from every angle that it can sometimes feel like a nonfiction speculative physics book. <br /><br />Of course, these brief periods of academic writing are mind-bending and lead to some of the most breathtaking imagery I’ve ever read. In that way, Liu works the reader for their reward, making it all the more satisfying when the pieces all fall into place. I could almost feel my mind stretching to accommodate the baffling dimension-altering scenes that occur later in the novel. In this book Liu shows his hand as not only a master of hard sci-fi, but as an imaginative genius.<br /><br />As with the other novels, <i>Death’s End</i> is not going to be for everyone. It is conceptually challenging, it is often exceedingly pessimistic, and unyielding in its style. With that said, I think it would get my vote for favorite sci-fi trilogy for those exact reasons. <i>Death’s End</i> ramps up the tension, pace, and action present in the earlier installments and then brings it all to a satisfying ending. If you’re a sci-fi fan, you owe it to yourself to read this trilogy! <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1157053804?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three-Body Problem Review</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1805158377?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forest Review</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15421449665755418254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2124628761" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2124628761" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2124628761?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=here-be-aliens">here-be-aliens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2124628761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9057591343608811842"><i>Review for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1532777152?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Three Body Problem</a><br/>Review for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1540591420?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dark Forest</a></i><br/><br/>After being disappointed by <i>The Dark Forest</i> I was a bit apprehensive of diving into <i>Death's End</i>, the final installment of the <i>Remembrance of Earth's Past</i> trilogy. Would the characters still be two-dimensional (well some would in this book, but for very different reasons)? Would the story dominate all other aspects of the book that would merely service the story instead of being ends unto themselves? Would I care about anyone in the book?</span>
  <span id="freeText9057591343608811842" style="display:none"><i>Review for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1532777152?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three Body Problem</a><br />Review for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1540591420?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Forest</a></i><br /><br />After being disappointed by <i>The Dark Forest</i> I was a bit apprehensive of diving into <i>Death's End</i>, the final installment of the <i>Remembrance of Earth's Past</i> trilogy. Would the characters still be two-dimensional (well some would in this book, but for very different reasons)? Would the story dominate all other aspects of the book that would merely service the story instead of being ends unto themselves? Would I care about anyone in the book? There were many weak areas that needed to be firmed up for this book to return the quality of <i>The Three Body Problem</i>.<br /><br />Thankfully this book recovered from the stumble that was <i>The Dark Forest</i>. I found the characters, while not stupendous, much improved in terms of depth and complexity. There were actually female characters that were not merely means to the ends of a male character. Cixin actually seemed to put some reasonable effort into developing his characters and the story was much improved for it. We got to see characters we had some emotional connection with and understanding of react to the many advances in human society they encounter over the epochs this book spans. I think having developed characters really enhanced the strongest part of Cixin's writing: his vision of the future.<br /><br />It is here where Cixin really shines, even in <i>The Dark Forest</i>. He just has this great knack for extrapolating how changes in technology and knowledge/awareness of the universe would impact human society, beliefs, and organization. We see vast range of different human responses to the challenges a big scary universe throws at them from tunneling underground on Earth to maintaining a mutually assured destruction stance with the Trisolarians to developing a constellation of city satellites in the shadow of the gas giants. Cixin examines how these changes impacted society and human organization in very creative and inventive ways without coming off as too fantastical. His takes on the far future were really intriguing and well thought out.<br /><br />Also, and maybe this is because we returned to Ken Liu as the translator, the writing just seemed crisper and clearer. The overall reading experience was just better than <i>The Dark Forest</i>. In fact this book had one of my favorite sections from the series. A fairy tale that coded some very important information was both beautifully written, well crafted, and hinted towards very important revelations key to the story.<br /><br />Overall this was a very enjoyable read. I did think the ending was a bit weak and felt rushed but it was a marked improvement over <i>The Dark Forest</i>. and on par with <i>The Three Body Problem</i> While not strong in all areas of writing the big ideas and imaginative realization of the future is enough to make the reading experience quite engaging and enjoyable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9057591343608811842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1751689113" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1751689113" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36819318-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485843897p2/36819318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1751689113?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/36819318-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1751689113" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15917173292296964480">Cixin Liu is massive in China. After the English publication of his brilliant <i>Three Body Problem</i> trilogy he deserves to be massive everywhere else.<br/><br/><i>Death's End</i> - the final book in the trilogy - is grand, universe-spanning SF of the most mind-expanding style. Liu throws around mind-blowing physics ideas like characters in potboiler fantasy novels chuck magic spells, and each one is a crystal ball's view into a world of scientific wonder. This book genuinely makes me want to study physics, just so </span>
  <span id="freeText15917173292296964480" style="display:none">Cixin Liu is massive in China. After the English publication of his brilliant <i>Three Body Problem</i> trilogy he deserves to be massive everywhere else.<br /><br /><i>Death's End</i> - the final book in the trilogy - is grand, universe-spanning SF of the most mind-expanding style. Liu throws around mind-blowing physics ideas like characters in potboiler fantasy novels chuck magic spells, and each one is a crystal ball's view into a world of scientific wonder. This book genuinely makes me want to study physics, just so I can enjoy even more fully the stunning concepts Liu bases much of his narrative upon. This is a story of real originality, leading to fascinating and thought-provoking conclusions about the very nature of our universe, and the ways in which it could have been influenced, formed and abused by the presence of intelligent life.<br /><br /><i>Death's End</i> continues on where <i>The Dark Forest</i> left off. Dark forest deterrence - the threat of a broadcast that will reveal the locations of Earth and Trilsolaris to the indiscriminately genocidal forces that lurk in the wider galaxy - keeps humanity and Trisolaris in an uneasy dance of mutually assured destruction. the ongoing deterrence era, as it has become known, has allowed a flowering of Earth technology under the influence of Trisolaran ideas. The Trisolarans themselves seem to have embraced earth culture and are producing movies, art and other cultural products that bear the marks of being inspired by Earth. <br /><br />Luo Ji, the character from <i>the Dark Forest</i> who invented dark forest deterrence, is now the person known as The Swordholder- the human being with final say over the broadcast of the civilization ending message to the galaxy.<br /><br />Into this story comes Cheng Xin, a young physics graduate who is recruited in the earlier crisis era to assist in a desperate attempt to spy on the incoming Trisolaran fleet. We follow Cheng Xin as she leaps through time via hibernation, becoming an important fulcrum on which decisions that could save or end the future of Earth and humanity turn. We follow as she is nominated to be the next Swordholder, the weight of humanity's survival upon her, and as she witnesses her world's ongoing struggle with Trisolaris.<br /><br />There are many surprises here, and I was genuinely blindsided several times by the narrative, so I won't say anything further about what happens other than noting that it's an exciting journey and Liu keeps up both the tension and the flow of the novel.<br /><br />This isn't however, a perfect novel. There are some great ideas in here, and they are sold really well, but towards the end of the book I began to enjoy it a little less.<br /><br />Cheng Xin seems to go from massive calamity to massive calamity, progressively becoming emptier inside as she becomes more responsible for the terrifying fate facing humanity. After the third or so kick in the guts for this character I began to wonder why the author had it in for her, denying her any chance of happiness and by doing so forcing the reader along on her sad ride. At every turn plot resolutions that could bring some light to the story are whipped away, leaving Xin falling into the abyss again and denying the reader resolution of plot arcs that travel all the way through the hundreds of pages of the novel.<br /><br />Furthermore, near the end of the book a shattering calamity occurs (a very interesting calamity), but the description of it goes for pages and pages until it begins to grate, annoyingly delaying the painfully inevitable escape of two of the story's characters. Considering how much of the story was left, it seemed obvious they would escape, and this was the only part of the book where I skimmed a bit to get to the next interesting section.<br /><br />From this point to the book's finale I was less impressed than I had been. <i>Death's End</i> is still more than worth your time - it's a novel of grand scope and fierce intelligence - but overall I was a little disappointed by the ending. I feel Liu got lost in his magnificent imaginings, and forgot that the end of a trilogy might require a bigger emotional payoff for the reader than what felt like the results of a very, very interesting thought experiment. <br /><br />As it stands though, this is still a fabulous novel, a gripping read that (mostly) kept me up late at night, desperate to discover what was going to happen next. SF of this calibre is rare, and Liu's consistently brilliant trilogy is a landmark in the genre, up there with Reynolds' <i>Revelation Space</i> stories and the <i>Hyperion</i> quartet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15917173292296964480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6762269197385497579">Seriously I don't know how to comment on this book. I am probably overly excited right now and still in that dramatical mood, just want to shout WoWwowwow. What a mind the author has! Cixin should have been a theoretical physicist rather than an engineer!<br/><br/> After reading this, now cixin liu has reached a place in my rank as the same as Asimov, Clark and the best of Orson Card. Especially considering I read most of those sci-fi giant books as a teenager, and now cixin still satisfied my grown-up ta</span>
  <span id="freeText6762269197385497579" style="display:none">Seriously I don't know how to comment on this book. I am probably overly excited right now and still in that dramatical mood, just want to shout WoWwowwow. What a mind the author has! Cixin should have been a theoretical physicist rather than an engineer!<br /><br /> After reading this, now cixin liu has reached a place in my rank as the same as Asimov, Clark and the best of Orson Card. Especially considering I read most of those sci-fi giant books as a teenager, and now cixin still satisfied my grown-up taste for overly complicated things and blew my mind away. That gotta be a lot harder. <br /><br />The author's writing skill is much better in this book comparing the first two in the trilogy. The first punch hits like 1/3 into the book, and there is an even bigger turn later which will leave you in such a trance state of mind. <br />The book also fills with fresh, imaginary details, some are really abstract yet very believable. The social aspect of the book reminds me Heinlein. Oddly enough, the author also shares the same nationalism inclination as Heinlein, and has a sharp criticism towards democracy. This might be due to the author's Chinese origin. I wonder whether this is the reason why Puppies love him. Well, they will love him even more after this book. <br /><br />Although I don't love the main character, Cheng Xin, I don't hate her either. She is like Daenerys Targaryen trapped in a gloomy hostile situation and there are no dragons this time. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1768209607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2410051115127567641">It is very hard to find a more mind-blowing book than this one. Death's End builds and builds and builds until it becomes an explosion of ideas, of space traveled, of time past in a very Stapledonian style. But, bare in mind that the main story line that begins in the first pages of the first volume ends at about 50% mark and the last 350 pages are in fact, like in The Return of the King, a very long finale. Although it's very hard to judge some aspects of this book without reading much more Chi</span>
  <span id="freeText2410051115127567641" style="display:none">It is very hard to find a more mind-blowing book than this one. Death's End builds and builds and builds until it becomes an explosion of ideas, of space traveled, of time past in a very Stapledonian style. But, bare in mind that the main story line that begins in the first pages of the first volume ends at about 50% mark and the last 350 pages are in fact, like in The Return of the King, a very long finale. Although it's very hard to judge some aspects of this book without reading much more Chinese speculative fiction, I will dare to say that the characters are clearly not the strong point of Cixin Liu writings. But this hardly matters when the ideas are in fact his real characters. And for a reader with an open mind, inhibiting an (scientific) idea can be as a profound and life changing experience as living inside the mind of Rodion Raskolnikov.</span>
